mw-body-content">\n<div class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-good-star"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Good_articles" title="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information."><img alt="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information." data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/19px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/29px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/39px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 2x" width="19"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Earth\'s magnetic field</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Magnetic field that extends from the Earth\'s inner core to where it meets the solar wind</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="717" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif/220px-Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif/330px-Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif/440px-Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geodynamo_Between_Reversals.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Computer simulation of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s field in a period of normal polarity between reversals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selfconsistent_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-selfconsistent-1">[1]</a></sup> The lines represent magnetic field lines, blue when the field points towards the center and yellow when away. The rotation axis of the Earth is centered and vertical. The dense clusters of lines are within the Earth\'s core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glatzmaier_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glatzmaier-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><b>Earth\'s magnetic field</b>, also known as the <b>geomagnetic field</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> that extends from the <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">Earth\'s interior</a> out into space, where it interacts with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, a stream of <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">charged particles</a> emanating from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. The magnetic field is generated by <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric currents</a> due to the motion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convection_current" title="Convection current">convection currents</a> of molten iron in the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer core</a>: these convection currents are caused by heat escaping from the core, a natural process called a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">geodynamo</a>. The magnitude of the Earth\'s magnetic field  at its surface ranges from 25 to 65&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">microteslas</a> (0.25 to 0.65&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeomagneticReferenceField_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeomagneticReferenceField-3">[3]</a></sup> As an approximation, it is represented by a field of a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">magnetic dipole</a> currently tilted at an angle of about 11 degrees with respect to <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth\'s rotation">Earth\'s rotational axis</a>, as if there were a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_magnet" title="Bar magnet">bar magnet</a> placed at that angle at the center of the Earth. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_geomagnetic_pole" title="North geomagnetic pole">North geomagnetic pole</a>, currently located near Greenland in the northern hemisphere, is actually the south pole of the Earth\'s magnetic field, and conversely.\n</p><p>While the North and South <a href="/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies" title="Poles of astronomical bodies">magnetic poles</a> are usually located near the geographic poles, they slowly and continuously move over geological time scales, but sufficiently slowly for ordinary <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compasses</a> to remain useful for navigation. However, at irregular intervals averaging several hundred thousand years, <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">the Earth\'s field reverses</a> and the North and <a href="/wiki/South_Magnetic_Pole" title="South Magnetic Pole">South Magnetic Poles</a> respectively, abruptly switch places. These reversals of the <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_pole" title="Geomagnetic pole">geomagnetic poles</a> leave a record in rocks that are of value to <a href="/wiki/Paleomagnetism" title="Paleomagnetism">paleomagnetists</a> in calculating geomagnetic fields in the past. Such information in turn is helpful in studying the motions of continents and ocean floors in the process of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> is the region above the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> that is defined by the extent of the Earth\'s magnetic field in space. It extends several tens of thousands of kilometers into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>, protecting the Earth from the charged particles of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> that would otherwise strip away the upper atmosphere, including the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> that protects the Earth from <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet#Harmful_effects" title="Ultraviolet">harmful ultraviolet radiation</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Biomagnetism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">Significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Earth\'s magnetic field serves to deflect most of the solar wind, whose charged particles would otherwise strip away the ozone layer that protects the Earth from harmful ultraviolet radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> One stripping mechanism is for gas to be caught in bubbles of magnetic field, which are ripped off by solar winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Calculations of the loss of carbon dioxide from the atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, resulting from scavenging of ions by the solar wind, indicate that the dissipation of the magnetic field of Mars caused a near total loss <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">of its atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The study of past magnetic field of the Earth is known as paleomagnetism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McElhinny2000_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-McElhinny2000-8">[8]</a></sup> The polarity of the Earth\'s magnetic field is recorded in <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversals" title="Geomagnetic reversals">reversals of the field</a> are thus detectable as "stripes" centered on <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Seafloor_spreading" title="Seafloor spreading">sea floor</a> is spreading, while the stability of the geomagnetic poles between reversals has allowed paleomagnetists to track the past motion of continents. Reversals also provide the basis for <a href="/wiki/Magnetostratigraphy" title="Magnetostratigraphy">magnetostratigraphy</a>, a way of <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">dating</a> rocks and sediments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The field also magnetizes the crust, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_anomalies" title="Magnetic anomalies">magnetic anomalies</a> can be used to search for deposits of metal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ores" title="Ores">ores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Humans have used compasses for direction finding since the 11th century A.D. and for navigation since the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">magnetic declination</a> does shift with time, this wandering is slow enough that a simple compass remains useful for navigation. Using <a href="/wiki/Magnetoreception" title="Magnetoreception">magnetoreception</a> various other organisms, ranging from some types of bacteria to pigeons, use the Earth\'s magnetic field for orientation and navigation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At any location, the Earth\'s magnetic field can be represented by a three-dimensional vector. A typical procedure for measuring its direction is to use a compass to determine the direction of magnetic North. Its angle relative to true North is the <i>declination</i> (<span class="texhtml"><var>D</var></span>) or <i>variation</i>. Facing magnetic North, the angle the field makes with the horizontal is the <i>inclination</i> (<span class="texhtml"><var>I</var></span>) or <i><a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dip" title="Magnetic dip">magnetic dip</a></i>. The <i>intensity</i> (<span class="texhtml"><var>F</var></span>) of the field is proportional to the force it exerts on a magnet. Another common representation is in <span class="texhtml"><var>X</var></span> (North), <span class="texhtml"><var>Y</var></span> (East) and <span class="texhtml"><var>Z</var></span> (Down) coordinates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch2-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="175" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg/500px-XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg/750px-XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg/1000px-XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:XYZ-DIS_magnetic_field_coordinates.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common coordinate systems used for representing the Earth\'s magnetic field.</div></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity">Intensity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Intensity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The intensity of the field is often measured in <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss (G)</a>, but is generally reported in nanoteslas (nT), with 1&#160;G = 100,000&#160;nT. A nanotesla is also referred to as a gamma (&#947;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGDC_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGDC-13">[13]</a></sup>   The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">tesla</a> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> unit of the magnetic field, <b>B</b>.  The Earth\'s field ranges between approximately 25,000 and 65,000&#160;nT (0.25&#8211;0.65&#160;G). By comparison, a strong <a href="/wiki/Refrigerator_magnet" title="Refrigerator magnet">refrigerator magnet</a> has a field of about 10,000,000 nanoteslas (100&#160;G).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A map of intensity contours is called an <i>isodynamic chart</i>. As the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field#Geographical_variation" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">World Magnetic Model</a> shows, the intensity tends to decrease from the poles to the equator. A minimum intensity occurs in the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Anomaly" title="South Atlantic Anomaly">South Atlantic Anomaly</a> over South America while there are maxima over northern Canada, Siberia, and the coast of Antarctica south of Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renamed_from_2010_on_20131022170733_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-renamed_from_2010_on_20131022170733-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclination">Inclination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Inclination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dip" title="Magnetic dip">Magnetic dip</a></div>\n<p>The inclination is given by an angle that can assume values between -90&#176; (up) to 90&#176; (down). In the northern hemisphere, the field points downwards. It is straight down at the <a href="/wiki/North_Magnetic_Pole" title="North Magnetic Pole">North Magnetic Pole</a> and rotates upwards as the latitude decreases until it is horizontal (0&#176;) at the magnetic equator. It continues to rotate upwards until it is straight up at the South Magnetic Pole. Inclination can be measured with a <a href="/wiki/Dip_circle" title="Dip circle">dip circle</a>.\n</p><p>An <i>isoclinic chart</i> (map of inclination contours) for the Earth\'s magnetic field is shown <a href="#Geographical_variation">below</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Declination">Declination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Declination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">Magnetic declination</a></div>\n<p>Declination is positive for an eastward deviation of the field relative to true north. It can be estimated by comparing the magnetic north/south heading on a compass with the direction of a <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">celestial pole</a>. Maps typically include information on the declination as an angle or a small diagram showing the relationship between magnetic north and true north. Information on declination for a region can be represented by a chart with isogonic lines (contour lines with each line representing a fixed declination).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_variation">Geographical variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geographical variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<center>\n<p>Components of the Earth\'s magnetic field at the surface from the <a href="/wiki/World_Magnetic_Model" title="World Magnetic Model">World Magnetic Model</a> for 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renamed_from_2010_on_20131022170733_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-renamed_from_2010_on_20131022170733-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</center>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 234px"><div style="width: 234px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 232px;"><div style="margin:0.16666666666667px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf"><img alt="" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf/page1-348px-World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf/page1-524px-World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf/page1-698px-World_Magnetic_Field_2015.pdf.jpg 2x" width="232"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Intensity\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 234px"><div style="width: 234px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 232px;"><div style="margin:0.16666666666667px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf"><img alt="" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf/page1-348px-World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf/page1-524px-World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf/page1-698px-World_Magnetic_Inclination_2015.pdf.jpg 2x" width="232"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Inclination\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 234px"><div style="width: 234px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 232px;"><div style="margin:0.16666666666667px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf"><img alt="" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf/page1-348px-World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf/page1-524px-World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf/page1-698px-World_Magnetic_Declination_2015.pdf.jpg 2x" width="232"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Declination\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dipolar_approximation">Dipolar approximation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dipolar approximation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetisme.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Geomagnetisme.svg/220px-Geomagnetisme.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Geomagnetisme.svg/330px-Geomagnetisme.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Geomagnetisme.svg/440px-Geomagnetisme.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetisme.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The variation between magnetic north (N<sub>m</sub>) and "true" north (N<sub>g</sub>).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dipole_model_of_the_Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Dipole model of the Earth\'s magnetic field">Dipole model of the Earth\'s magnetic field</a></div>\n<p>Near the surface of the Earth, its magnetic field can be closely approximated by the field of a magnetic dipole positioned at the center of the Earth and tilted at an angle of about 11&#176; with respect to the rotational axis of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGDC_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGDC-13">[13]</a></sup> The dipole is roughly equivalent to a powerful bar <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnet</a>, with its south pole pointing towards the geomagnetic North Pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This may seem surprising, but the north pole of a magnet is so defined because, if allowed to rotate freely, it points roughly northward (in the geographic sense). Since the north pole of a magnet attracts the south poles of other magnets and repels the north poles, it must be attracted to the south pole of Earth\'s magnet. The dipolar field accounts for 80&#8211;90% of the field in most locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch2_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch2-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_poles">Magnetic poles<span id="Poles"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Magnetic poles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_pole" title="Geomagnetic pole">Geomagnetic pole</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="445" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg/220px-Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg/330px-Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg/440px-Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_North_Pole_Positions_2015.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The movement of Earth\'s North Magnetic Pole across the Canadian arctic.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, the north and south poles of a magnet were first defined by the Earth\'s magnetic field, not vice versa, since one of the first uses for a magnet was as a compass needle. A magnet\'s  North pole is defined as the pole that is attracted by the Earth\'s North Magnetic Pole when the magnet is suspended so it can turn freely. Since opposite poles attract, the North Magnetic Pole of the Earth is really the south pole of its magnetic field (the place where the field is directed downward into the Earth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serway_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serway-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emiliani_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emiliani-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manners_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manners-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyperphysics_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyperphysics-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The positions of the magnetic poles can be defined in at least two ways: locally or globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The local definition is the point where the magnetic field is vertical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This can be determined by measuring the inclination. The inclination of the Earth\'s field is 90&#176; (downwards) at the North Magnetic Pole and -90&#176; (upwards) at the South Magnetic Pole. The two poles wander independently of each other and are not directly opposite each other on the globe.  Movements of up to 40 kilometres (25&#160;mi) per year have been observed for the North Magnetic Pole. Over the last 180 years, the North Magnetic Pole has been migrating northwestward, from Cape Adelaide in the <a href="/wiki/Boothia_Peninsula" title="Boothia Peninsula">Boothia Peninsula</a> in 1831 to 600 kilometres (370&#160;mi) from <a href="/wiki/Resolute_Bay" title="Resolute Bay">Resolute Bay</a> in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inconstant_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-inconstant-23">[23]</a></sup> The <i>magnetic equator</i> is the line where the inclination is zero (the magnetic field is horizontal).\n</p><p>The global definition of the Earth\'s field is based on a mathematical model. If a line is drawn through the center of the Earth, parallel to the moment of the best-fitting magnetic dipole, the two positions where it intersects the Earth\'s surface are called the North and South geomagnetic poles. If the Earth\'s magnetic field were perfectly dipolar, the geomagnetic poles and magnetic dip poles would coincide and compasses would point towards them. However, the Earth\'s field has a significant <a href="/wiki/Multipole_expansion" title="Multipole expansion">non-dipolar</a> contribution, so the poles do not coincide and compasses do not generally point at either.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Magnetosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetosphere_Levels.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="330" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Magnetosphere_Levels.svg/220px-Magnetosphere_Levels.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Magnetosphere_Levels.svg/330px-Magnetosphere_Levels.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Magnetosphere_Levels.svg/440px-Magnetosphere_Levels.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetosphere_Levels.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An artist\'s rendering of the structure of a magnetosphere. 1)&#160;Bow shock. 2)&#160;Magnetosheath. 3)&#160;Magnetopause. 4)&#160;Magnetosphere. 5)&#160;Northern tail lobe. 6)&#160;Southern tail lobe. 7)&#160;Plasmasphere.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</a></div>\n<p>Earth\'s magnetic field, predominantly dipolar at its surface, is distorted further out by the solar wind. This is a stream of charged particles leaving the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> and accelerating to a speed of 200 to 1000&#160;kilometres per second. They carry with them a magnetic field, the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_magnetic_field" title="Interplanetary magnetic field">interplanetary magnetic field</a> (IMF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MerrillMagnetosphere_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillMagnetosphere-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The solar wind exerts a pressure, and if it could reach Earth\'s atmosphere it would erode it. However, it is kept away by the pressure of the Earth\'s magnetic field. The <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a>, the area where the pressures balance, is the boundary of the magnetosphere. Despite its name, the magnetosphere is asymmetric, with the sunward side being about 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius">Earth radii</a> out but the other side stretching out in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a> that extends beyond 200&#160;Earth radii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParksIntro_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParksIntro-25">[25]</a></sup>\nSunward of the magnetopause is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a>, the area where the solar wind slows abruptly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MerrillMagnetosphere_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillMagnetosphere-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Inside the magnetosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Plasmasphere" title="Plasmasphere">plasmasphere</a>, a donut-shaped region containing low-energy charged particles, or <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>. This region begins at a height of 60&#160;km, extends up to 3 or 4 Earth radii, and includes the ionosphere. This region rotates with the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParksIntro_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParksIntro-25">[25]</a></sup> There are also two concentric tire-shaped regions, called the <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">Van Allen radiation belts</a>, with high-energy ions (energies from 0.1 to 10&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electron volts</a> (MeV)). The inner belt is 1&#8211;2&#160;Earth radii out while the outer belt is at 4&#8211;7&#160;Earth radii. The plasmasphere and Van Allen belts have partial overlap, with the extent of overlap varying greatly with solar activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As well as deflecting the solar wind, the Earth\'s magnetic field deflects <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, high-energy charged particles that are mostly from outside the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Many cosmic rays are kept out of the Solar System by the Sun\'s magnetosphere, or <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>) By contrast, astronauts on the Moon risk exposure to radiation. Anyone who had been on the Moon\'s surface during a particularly violent solar eruption in 2005 would have received a lethal dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MerrillMagnetosphere_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillMagnetosphere-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the charged particles do get into the magnetosphere. These spiral around field lines, bouncing back and forth between the poles several times per second. In addition, positive ions slowly drift westward and negative ions drift eastward, giving rise to a <a href="/wiki/Ring_current" title="Ring current">ring current</a>. This current reduces the magnetic field at the Earth\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MerrillMagnetosphere_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillMagnetosphere-24">[24]</a></sup> Particles that penetrate the ionosphere and collide with the atoms there give rise to the lights of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurorae</a> and also emit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParksIntro_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-ParksIntro-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The varying conditions in the magnetosphere, known as <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a>, are largely driven by solar activity. If the solar wind is weak, the magnetosphere expands; while if it is strong, it compresses the magnetosphere and more of it gets in. Periods of particularly intense activity, called <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storms</a>, can occur when a <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejection</a> erupts above the Sun and sends a shock wave through the Solar System. Such a wave can take just two days to reach the Earth. Geomagnetic storms can cause a lot of disruption; <a href="/wiki/Halloween_solar_storms,_2003" title="Halloween solar storms, 2003">the "Halloween" storm of 2003</a> damaged more than a third of NASA\'s satellites. The largest documented storm occurred in 1859. It induced currents strong enough to short out telegraph lines, and aurorae were reported as far south as Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MerrillMagnetosphere_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillMagnetosphere-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_dependence">Time dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Time dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short-term_variations">Short-term variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Short-term variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg/220px-Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg/330px-Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg/440px-Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_Storm_Oct_2003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Background</b>: a set of traces from magnetic observatories showing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_storm" title="Magnetic storm">magnetic storm</a> in 2000.<br/> <b>Globe</b>: map showing locations of observatories and contour lines giving horizontal magnetic intensity in <a href="/wiki/Micro-" title="Micro-">&#956;</a> <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The geomagnetic field changes on time scales from milliseconds to millions of years. Shorter time scales mostly arise from currents in the ionosphere (<a href="/wiki/Ionospheric_dynamo_region" title="Ionospheric dynamo region">ionospheric dynamo region</a>) and magnetosphere, and some changes can be traced to geomagnetic storms or daily variations in currents. Changes over time scales of a year or more mostly reflect changes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_interior" title="Earth\'s interior">Earth\'s interior</a>, particularly the iron-rich <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch2_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch2-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Frequently, the Earth\'s magnetosphere is hit by <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> causing geomagnetic storms, provoking displays of aurorae. The short-term instability of the magnetic field is measured with the <a href="/wiki/K-index" title="K-index">K-index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Data from <a href="/wiki/THEMIS" title="THEMIS">THEMIS</a> show that the magnetic field, which interacts with the solar wind, is reduced when the magnetic orientation is aligned between Sun and Earth &#8211; opposite to the previous hypothesis. During forthcoming solar storms, this could result in <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">blackouts</a> and disruptions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_satellite" title="Artificial satellite">artificial satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_variation">Secular variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Secular variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_secular_variation" title="Geomagnetic secular variation">Geomagnetic secular variation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="351" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif/220px-Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif/330px-Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_Magnetic_Field_Declination_from_1590_to_1990.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated declination contours by year, 1590 to 1990 (click to see variation).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="715" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg/220px-Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg/330px-Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg/440px-Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetic_axial_dipole_strength.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Strength of the axial dipole component of Earth\'s magnetic field from 1600 to 2020.</div></div></div>\n<p>Changes in Earth\'s magnetic field on a time scale of a year or more are referred to as <i>secular variation</i>. Over hundreds of years, magnetic declination is observed to vary over tens of degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch2_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch2-12">[12]</a></sup> The animation shows how global declinations have changed over the last few centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-declination_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-declination-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The direction and intensity of the dipole change over time. Over the last two centuries the dipole strength has been decreasing at a rate of about 6.3% per century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch2_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch2-12">[12]</a></sup> At this rate of decrease, the field would be negligible in about 1600 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSC_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSC-32">[32]</a></sup> However, this strength is about average for the last 7 thousand years, and the current rate of change is not unusual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constable_dipole_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Constable_dipole-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A prominent feature in the non-dipolar part of the secular variation is a <i>westward drift</i> at a rate of about 0.2 degrees per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSC_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-GSC-32">[32]</a></sup> This drift is not the same everywhere and has varied over time. The globally averaged drift has been westward since about 1400 AD but eastward between about 1000 AD and 1400 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumberry_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumberry-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Changes that predate magnetic observatories are recorded in archaeological and geological materials. Such changes are referred to as <i>paleomagnetic secular variation</i> or <i>paleosecular variation (PSV)</i>. The records typically include long periods of small change with occasional large changes reflecting <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_excursion" title="Geomagnetic excursion">geomagnetic excursions</a> and reversals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TauxeCh1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-TauxeCh1-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_reversals">Magnetic field reversals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Magnetic field reversals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="380" decoding="async" height="545" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg/180px-Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg/270px-Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg/360px-Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geomagnetic_polarity_late_Cenozoic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geomagnetic polarity during the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cenozoic_Era" title="Cenozoic Era">Cenozoic Era</a>. Dark areas denote periods where the polarity matches today\'s polarity, light areas denote periods where that polarity is reversed.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">Geomagnetic reversal</a></div>\n<p>Although generally Earth\'s field is approximately dipolar, with an axis that is nearly aligned with the rotational axis, occasionally the North and South <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_poles" title="Geomagnetic poles">geomagnetic poles</a> trade places. Evidence for these <i>geomagnetic reversals</i> can be found in <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalts</a>, sediment cores taken from the ocean floors, and seafloor magnetic anomalies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Reversals occur nearly randomly in time, with intervals between reversals ranging from less than 0.1 million years to as much as 50 million years. The most recent geomagnetic reversal, called the <a href="/wiki/Brunhes%E2%80%93Matuyama_reversal" title="Brunhes&#8211;Matuyama reversal">Brunhes&#8211;Matuyama reversal</a>, occurred about 780,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inconstant_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-inconstant-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch5_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch5-37">[37]</a></sup> A related phenomenon, a geomagnetic excursion, amounts to an incomplete reversal, with no change in polarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMexcursion_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMexcursion-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Laschamp_event" title="Laschamp event">Laschamp event</a> is an example of an excursion, it having occurred during the last ice age (41,000 years ago).\n</p><p>The past magnetic field is recorded mostly by <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_mineralogy#Strongly_magnetic_minerals" title="Magnetic mineralogy">strongly magnetic minerals</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_oxides" title="Iron oxides">iron oxides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a>, that can carry a permanent magnetic moment. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remanent_magnetization" title="Remanent magnetization">remanent magnetization</a>, or <i>remanence</i>, can be acquired in more than one way. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_flow" title="Lava flow">lava flows</a>, the direction of the field is "frozen" in small minerals as they cool, giving rise to a <a href="/wiki/Thermoremanent_magnetization" title="Thermoremanent magnetization">thermoremanent magnetization</a>. In sediments, the orientation of magnetic particles acquires a slight bias towards the magnetic field as they are deposited on an ocean floor or lake bottom. This is called <i>detrital remanent magnetization</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McElhinny2000_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-McElhinny2000-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thermoremanent magnetization is the main source of the magnetic anomalies around mid-ocean ridges. As the seafloor spreads, <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> wells up from the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, cools to form new basaltic crust on both sides of the ridge, and is carried away from it by seafloor spreading. As it cools, it records the direction of the Earth\'s field. When the Earth\'s field reverses, new basalt records the reversed direction. The result is a series of stripes that are symmetric about the ridge. A ship towing a magnetometer on the surface of the ocean can detect these stripes and infer the age of the ocean floor below. This provides information on the rate at which seafloor has spread in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McElhinny2000_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-McElhinny2000-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">Radiometric dating</a> of lava flows has been used to establish a <i>geomagnetic polarity time scale</i>, part of which is shown in the image. This forms the basis of <a href="/wiki/Magnetostratigraphy" title="Magnetostratigraphy">magnetostratigraphy</a>, a geophysical correlation technique that can be used to date both sedimentary and volcanic sequences as well as the seafloor magnetic anomalies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McElhinny2000_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-McElhinny2000-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies of lava flows on <a href="/wiki/Steens_Mountain" title="Steens Mountain">Steens Mountain</a>, Oregon, indicate that the magnetic field could have shifted at a rate of up to 6 degrees per day at some time in Earth\'s history, which significantly challenges the popular understanding of how the Earth\'s magnetic field works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-40">[40]</a></sup> This finding was later attributed to unusual rock magnetic properties of the lava flow under study, not rapid field change, by one of the original authors of the 1995 study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epsl.com_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-epsl.com-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Temporary dipole tilt variations that take the dipole axis across the equator and then back to the original polarity are known as <i>excursions</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMexcursion_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMMexcursion-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_appearance">Earliest appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Earliest appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Paleomagnetic studies of <a href="/wiki/Paleoarchean" title="Paleoarchean">Paleoarchean</a> lava in Australia and <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(geology)" title="Conglomerate (geology)">conglomerate</a> in South Africa have concluded that the magnetic field has been present since at least about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=3,450">3,450</a>&#160;million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarduno2009_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tarduno2009-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png/220px-Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png/330px-Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png/440px-Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brunhes_geomagnetism_western_US.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variations in virtual axial dipole moment since the last reversal.</div></div></div>\n<p>At present, the overall geomagnetic field is becoming weaker; the present strong deterioration corresponds to a 10&#8211;15% decline over the last 150 years and has accelerated in the past several years; geomagnetic intensity has declined almost continuously from a maximum 35% above the modern value achieved approximately 2,000 years ago. The rate of decrease and the current strength are within the normal range of variation, as shown by the record of past magnetic fields recorded in rocks.\n</p><p>The nature of Earth\'s magnetic field is one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heteroscedastic" title="Heteroscedastic">heteroscedastic</a> fluctuation. An instantaneous measurement of it, or several measurements of it across the span of decades or centuries, are not sufficient to extrapolate an overall trend in the field strength. It has gone up and down in the past for unknown reasons. Also, noting the local intensity of the dipole field (or its fluctuation) is insufficient to characterize Earth\'s magnetic field as a whole, as it is not strictly a dipole field. The dipole component of Earth\'s field can diminish even while the total magnetic field remains the same or increases.\n</p><p>The Earth\'s magnetic north pole is drifting from northern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> with a presently accelerating rate&#8212;10 kilometres (6.2&#160;mi) per year at the beginning of the 20th century, up to 40 kilometres (25&#160;mi) per year in 2003,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inconstant_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-inconstant-23">[23]</a></sup> and since then has only accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_origin">Physical origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Physical origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">Dynamo theory</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Earth.27s_core_and_the_geodynamo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth\'s_core_and_the_geodynamo">Earth\'s core and the geodynamo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Earth\'s core and the geodynamo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Earth\'s magnetic field is believed to be generated by electric currents in the conductive iron alloys of its core, created by convection currents due to heat escaping from the core.  However the process is complex, and computer models that reproduce some of its features have only been developed in the last few decades.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outer_core_convection_rolls.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="278" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Outer_core_convection_rolls.jpg/220px-Outer_core_convection_rolls.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Outer_core_convection_rolls.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outer_core_convection_rolls.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A schematic illustrating the relationship between motion of conducting fluid, organized into rolls by the Coriolis force, and the magnetic field the motion generates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Earth and most of the planets in the Solar System, as well as the Sun and other stars, all generate magnetic fields through the motion of electrically <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conducting</a> fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-47">[47]</a></sup> The Earth\'s field originates in its core. This is a region of iron alloys extending to about 3400&#160;km (the radius of the Earth is 6370&#160;km). It is divided into a solid <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a>, with a radius of 1220&#160;km, and a liquid <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The motion of the liquid in the outer core is driven by heat flow from the inner core, which is about 6,000&#160;K (5,730&#160;&#176;C; 10,340&#160;&#176;F), to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core-mantle_boundary" title="Core-mantle boundary">core-mantle boundary</a>, which is about 3,800&#160;K (3,530&#160;&#176;C; 6,380&#160;&#176;F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>  The heat is generated by potential energy released by heavier materials sinking toward the core (<a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">planetary differentiation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iron_catastrophe" title="Iron catastrophe">iron catastrophe</a>) as well as decay of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> elements in the interior.  The pattern of flow is organized by the rotation of the Earth and the presence of the solid inner core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffett2000_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buffett2000-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mechanism by which the Earth generates a magnetic field is known as a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">dynamo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-47">[47]</a></sup> The magnetic field is generated by a feedback loop: current loops generate magnetic fields (<a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_circuital_law" title="Amp&#232;re\'s circuital law">Amp&#232;re\'s circuital law</a>); a changing magnetic field generates an electric field (<a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday\'s law of induction">Faraday\'s law</a>); and the electric and magnetic fields exert a force on the charges that are flowing in currents (the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">Lorentz force</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> These effects can be combined in a <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">partial differential equation</a> for the magnetic field called the <i>magnetic induction equation</i>,\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {B} }{\\partial t}}=\\eta \\nabla ^{2}\\mathbf {B} +\\nabla \\times (\\mathbf {u} \\times \\mathbf {B} ),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8711;<!-- &#8711; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8711;<!-- &#8711; --></mi>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">u</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {B} }{\\partial t}}=\\eta \\nabla ^{2}\\mathbf {B} +\\nabla \\times (\\mathbf {u} \\times \\mathbf {B} ),}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {B} }{\\partial t}}=\\eta \\nabla ^{2}\\mathbf {B} +\\nabla \\times (\\mathbf {u} \\times \\mathbf {B} ),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5f3a1294285c5cfc391c41dfc2ffe97520180644" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:29.514ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="texhtml"><b>u</b></span> is the velocity of the fluid; <span class="texhtml"><b>B</b></span> is the magnetic B-field; and <span class="texhtml">&#951;=1/&#963;&#956;</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_diffusivity" title="Magnetic diffusivity">magnetic diffusivity</a>, which is inversely proportional to the product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> <span class="texhtml">&#963;</span> and the <a href="/wiki/Permeability_(electromagnetism)" title="Permeability (electromagnetism)">permeability</a> <span class="texhtml">&#956;</span> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch8_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch8-52">[52]</a></sup>  The term <span class="texhtml">&#8706;<b>B</b>/&#8706;<i>t</i></span> is the time derivative of the field; <span class="texhtml">&#8711;<sup>2</sup></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Laplace_operator" title="Laplace operator">Laplace operator</a> and <span class="texhtml">&#8711;&#215;</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">curl operator</a>.\n</p><p>The first term on the right hand side of the induction equation is a <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> term. In a stationary fluid, the magnetic field declines and any concentrations of field spread out. If the Earth\'s dynamo shut off, the dipole part would disappear in a few tens of thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch8_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch8-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a perfect conductor (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\sigma =\\infty \\;}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8734;<!-- &#8734; --></mi>\n<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\sigma =\\infty \\;}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\sigma =\\infty \\;}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0631216f514459564536249e21be791a8d25e427" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.397ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>), there would be no diffusion. By <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz\'s law">Lenz\'s law</a>, any change in the magnetic field would be immediately opposed by currents, so the flux through a given volume of fluid could not change. As the fluid moved, the magnetic field would go with it. The theorem describing this effect is called the <i>frozen-in-field theorem</i>. Even in a fluid with a finite conductivity, new field is generated by stretching field lines as the fluid moves in ways that deform it. This process could go on generating new field indefinitely, were it not that as the magnetic field increases in strength, it resists fluid motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch8_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch8-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The motion of the fluid is sustained by <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>, motion driven by <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>. The temperature increases towards the center of the Earth, and the higher temperature of the fluid lower down makes it buoyant. This buoyancy is enhanced by chemical separation: As the core cools, some of the molten iron solidifies and is plated to the inner core. In the process, lighter elements are left behind in the fluid, making it lighter. This is called <i>compositional convection</i>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>, caused by the overall planetary rotation, tends to organize the flow into rolls aligned along the north-south polar axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffett2000_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buffett2000-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch8_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch8-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A dynamo can amplify a magnetic field, but it needs a "seed" field to get it started.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch8_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch8-52">[52]</a></sup> For the Earth, this could have been an external magnetic field. Early in its history the Sun went through a <a href="/wiki/T_Tauri_star" title="T Tauri star">T-Tauri phase</a> in which the solar wind would have had a magnetic field orders of magnitude larger than the present solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch10_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch10-53">[53]</a></sup> However, much of the field may have been screened out by the Earth\'s mantle. An alternative source is currents in the core-mantle boundary driven by chemical reactions or variations in thermal or electric conductivity. Such effects may still provide a small bias that are part of the boundary conditions for the geodynamo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMMch11_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMMch11-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The average magnetic field in the Earth\'s outer core was calculated to be 25 gausses, 50 times stronger than the field at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_models">Numerical models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Numerical models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Simulating the geodynamo requires numerically solving a set of nonlinear partial differential equations for the <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">magnetohydrodynamics</a> (MHD) of the Earth\'s interior. Simulation of the MHD equations is performed on a 3D grid of points and the fineness of the grid, which in part determines the realism of the solutions, is limited mainly by computer power. For decades, theorists were confined to creating <i>kinematic dynamo</i> computer models in which the fluid motion is chosen in advance and the effect on the magnetic field calculated. Kinematic dynamo theory was mainly a matter of trying different flow geometries and testing whether such geometries could sustain a dynamo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kono2002_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kono2002-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first <i>self-consistent</i> dynamo models, ones that determine both the fluid motions and the magnetic field, were developed by two groups in 1995, one in Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and one in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selfconsistent_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-selfconsistent-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The latter received attention because it successfully reproduced some of the characteristics of the Earth\'s field, including geomagnetic reversals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kono2002_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kono2002-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currents_in_the_ionosphere_and_magnetosphere">Currents in the ionosphere and magnetosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Currents in the ionosphere and magnetosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Electric currents induced in the ionosphere generate magnetic fields (ionospheric dynamo region). Such a field is always generated near where the atmosphere is closest to the Sun, causing daily alterations that can deflect surface magnetic fields by as much as one degree. Typical daily variations of field strength are about 25 nanoteslas (nT) (one part in 2000), with variations over a few seconds of typically around 1 nT (one part in 50,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement_and_analysis">Measurement and analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Measurement and analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection">Detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Earth\'s magnetic field strength was measured by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> in 1832<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and has been repeatedly measured since then, showing a relative decay of about 10% over the last 150 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magsat" title="Magsat">Magsat</a> satellite and later satellites have used 3-axis vector magnetometers to probe the 3-D structure of the Earth\'s magnetic field. The later <a href="/wiki/%C3%98rsted_(satellite)" title="&#216;rsted (satellite)">&#216;rsted satellite</a> allowed a comparison indicating a dynamic geodynamo in action that appears to be giving rise to an alternate pole under the Atlantic Ocean west of South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11948347_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11948347-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Governments sometimes operate units that specialize in measurement of the Earth\'s magnetic field. These are <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Geomagnetic_observatories&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Geomagnetic observatories (page does not exist)">geomagnetic observatories</a>, typically part of a national <a href="/wiki/Geological_survey" title="Geological survey">Geological survey</a>, for example the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Eskdalemuir_Observatory" title="Eskdalemuir Observatory">Eskdalemuir Observatory</a>. Such observatories can measure and forecast magnetic conditions such as magnetic storms that sometimes affect communications, electric power, and other human activities.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Intermagnet" title="Intermagnet">International Real-time Magnetic Observatory Network</a>, with over 100 interlinked geomagnetic observatories around the world, has been recording the Earth\'s magnetic field since 1991.\n</p><p>The military determines local geomagnetic field characteristics, in order to detect <i>anomalies</i> in the natural background that might be caused by a significant metallic object such as a submerged submarine. Typically, these <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_anomaly_detector" title="Magnetic anomaly detector">magnetic anomaly detectors</a> are flown in aircraft like the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod" title="Hawker Siddeley Nimrod">Nimrod</a> or towed as an instrument or an array of instruments from surface ships.\n</p><p>Commercially, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geophysical" title="Geophysical">geophysical</a> <a href="/wiki/Prospecting" title="Prospecting">prospecting</a> companies also use magnetic detectors to identify naturally occurring anomalies from <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> bodies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kursk_Magnetic_Anomaly" title="Kursk Magnetic Anomaly">Kursk Magnetic Anomaly</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crustal_magnetic_anomalies">Crustal magnetic anomalies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Crustal magnetic anomalies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_Field_Earth.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="291" data-file-width="568" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Magnetic_Field_Earth.png/220px-Magnetic_Field_Earth.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Magnetic_Field_Earth.png/330px-Magnetic_Field_Earth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Magnetic_Field_Earth.png/440px-Magnetic_Field_Earth.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_Field_Earth.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of short-wavelength features of Earth\'s magnetic field, attributed to lithospheric anomalies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometers</a> detect minute deviations in the Earth\'s magnetic field caused by iron <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a>, kilns, some types of stone structures, and even ditches and <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">middens</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_geophysics" title="Archaeological geophysics">archaeological geophysics</a>. Using magnetic instruments adapted from airborne magnetic anomaly detectors developed during World War II to detect submarines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> the magnetic variations across the ocean floor have been mapped. Basalt &#8212; the iron-rich, volcanic rock making up the ocean floor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> &#8212; contains a strongly magnetic mineral (magnetite) and can locally distort compass readings. The distortion was recognized by Icelandic mariners as early as the late 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kious_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kious-66">[66]</a></sup> More important, because the presence of magnetite gives the basalt measurable magnetic properties, these magnetic variations have provided another means to study the deep ocean floor. When newly formed rock cools, such magnetic materials record the Earth\'s magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kious_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kious-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical_models">Statistical models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Statistical models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Each measurement of the magnetic field is at a particular place and time. If an accurate estimate of the field at some other place and time is needed, the measurements must be converted to a model and the model used to make predictions.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spherical_harmonics">Spherical harmonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth%27s_magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Spherical harmonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Multipole_expansion" title="Multipole expansion">Multipole expansion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="265" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg/220px-Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg/330px-Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg/440px-Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spherical_harmonics_positive_negative.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic representation of spherical harmonics on a sphere and their nodal lines. <span class="texhtml"><var>P</var><sub>&#8467; <var>m</var></sub></span> is equal to 0 along <span class="texhtml"><var>m</var></span> <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">great circles</a> passing through the poles, and along <span class="texhtml">&#8467;-<var>m</var></span> circles of equal latitude. The function changes sign each &#8467;time it crosses one of these lines.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_four_charges.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/VFPt_four_charges.svg/220px-VFPt_four_charges.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/VFPt_four_charges.svg/330px-VFPt_four_charges.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/VFPt_four_charges.svg/440px-VFPt_four_charges.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_four_charges.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of a quadrupole field. This can also be constructed by moving two dipoles together.</div></div></div>\n<p>The most common way of analyzing the global variations in the Earth\'s magnetic field is to fit the measurements to a set of <a href="/wiki/Spherical_harmonics" title="Spherical harmonics">spherical harmonics</a>. This was first done by Carl Friedrich Gauss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace2003_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace2003-67">[67]</a></sup> Spherical harmonics are functions that oscillate over the surface of a sphere.
